This report is based on the findings of an appraisal missi.i to Chad in
January 1989 and a post-appraisal mission in November 1989. The appraisal
mission members included Rene Vaurs (mission leader), Amadou Cisse (public
works), Eugene Boostrom (health), Leslie Pean (small and micro-
enterprises), and Gabrielle Rooz (NGOs and project costing); the SDA team:
Alexandre Marc (coordinator). Jean-Luc Dubois and Antoine Simonpietri
(surveys), Andre Komenan (socioeconomic studies), Eileen Murray (NGOs and
programming); Taoufik Lahlou (urban development), and Jean-Claude Barneaud
(technology transfer), consultants. Katheryn Imboden, Antoine Degremont
(Swiss Development Cooperation), Dhuc Nguyen (UNCDF), and Bernard Wilder
(USAID) participated in the mission. This report was edited by Ms. Phyllis
Roos.

REPUBLIC OF CHAD
SOCIAL DEVELOPMENT ACTION PROJECT
CREDIT AND PROJECT SUMMARY
Borrower:            Republic of Chad
Beneficiaries:       Ministries of Higher Education, Labor and Employment,
Planning and Cooperation, Public Health, Regional
Urban Planning and Housing, and Social Affairs and
Women's Development.
Credit Amount:       SDR 10.4 million (US$13.4 million equivalent)
Terms:               Standard, with 40 years' maturity
Objectives:          The project aims to (a) stimulate employment
generation by increasing access to training facilities
and productive assets for poor and/or unemployed
people; (b) improve health and living conditions of
disadvantaged groups; and (c) reinforce the
Government's capacity to design and monitor programs
to improve social conditions. These objectives
address the immediate needs of the population groups
(a) still at risk as a consequence of the
socioeconomic disruptions of the past decade, and (b)
expected to be adversely affected by the transitional
effects associated with the adjustment process
recently initiated; they also address the need to
alleviate poverty, strengthen the formulation of
sectoral strategies for meeting basic needs, and
develop human resources over the long term.
Project
Description:         The project comprises the following components:
(a)  Stimulation of employment generation by providing
(i) credit assistance and management support for small
and micro-enterprises, (ii) technical assistance
tailored to their needs, and (iii) assistance to laid-
off public employees.
(b)  Improvement of primary health care and social services
by (i) restructuring and expanding the network of
these services in N'Djamena, including the
construction and operation of three integrated health-
social centers, three dispensaries and three maternity
centers; and (ii) strengthening the regional health
services in the Prefecture of Tandjile in the cotton
zone.



- iv -
(c) Improvement of N'Djamena's sanitation infrastructure
by (i) rehabilitating the drainage system in two
flood-prone areas where a high percentage of low-
income families live, and (ii) Improving public
hygiene through increased community participation and
use of labor-intensive public works techniques.
(d) Strengthening of social policy planning by (i)
undertaking small sample household surveys in the
cotton zone and three cities, an integrated nationwide
household survey, and socioeconomic studies; and (ii)
assisting the Government in applying the findings of
these surveys and studies, together with experience
gained in implementing the other components, to define
future policies and actions targeted at vulnerable
population groups.
Summary of Project Costs and Financing
(net of taxes and duties)

of Return:           Not applicable.
Project
Benefits:      The project would (a) enhance job creation by providinr,
15,000 jobs through labor-intensive activities and
assisting about 700 micro and small enterprises; (b) etpand
access to higher-quality health care and social services
for about 150.000 residents of N'Djamena at the end of the
project, double the utilization of the health centers in
N'Djamena and Tandjile (540,000 and 360,000 inhabitants,
respectively), and reduce health hazards for 85,000 persons
living in areas where the project will rehabilitate the
sanitation infrastructure; (c) test the sustainability and
replicability of innovative approaches to addressing social
issues, and contribute to the effective design of long-
term strategies in the social sectors, through applied
socioeconomic research combined with lessons drawn from
implementing specific action plans; and (d) help the
Government to promote community and private initiatives and
to collaborate more effectively with NGOs.
Project
Risks:          The project's main risks are related to the need to
effectively implement its diversified activities while
respecting its objectives that address the urgent needs of
disadvantaged groups. Measures to mitigate these risks
include: (a) definition of detailed annual action plans
and identification of the particular target beneficiary
groups, as opposed to financing activities to be identified
during project implementation; (b) refinement and expansion
of the techniques already effectively applied in Chad by
NGOs or other organizations; (c) the setting up of a
Project Coordination and Support Unit with financial
-  autonomy and full-time staff; and (d) provision of
substantial training and technical assistance inputs to
enable existing public services to operate more
efficiently. The project's multisectoral activities
require close monitoring and regular consultations among
all parties concerned, including the cofinanciers.



REPUBLIC OF CHAD
SOCIAL DEVELOPMENT ACTION PROJECT
I. INTRODUCTION
1.01      The Government of Chad has requested IDA financing to implement
a Social Development Action Project, prepared with IDA's assistance as an
integral part of a larger Government social action program referred to in
Chad as PADS ("Programme d'Actions pour le Developpement Social").
Comprising priority actions for meeting some of the basic needs of the
population groups still affected by the events that disrupted public
services and destroyed most of the country's physical and social
infrastructure over the past decade, this program aims not only to improve
the very low levels of basic services provided for those groups, but also
to stimulate employment generation and strengthen the Government's capacity
to design and monitor programs for ensuring a more efficient development of
the country's human resources. These actions are consistent with and
support the Government's development strategy geared to reconstruction and
the promotion of domestic production. They will also serve to mitigate the
transitional social impact of tLe adjustment process required to restore
the economic and financial viability of the cotton sector, improve the
country's public finances and economic management, and revamp sectoral
growth prospects in some key sectors   The PADS program marks the
transitional phase of the country'   :zovery effort from relief aid to
better-structured social welfare a- poverty alleviation programs targeted
at the most disadvantaged groups.
1.02      The PADS program is supported by a coordinated package of
external financial and technical assistance to the Government of Chad, and
is consistent with IDA's strategy to support the country's financial and
economic recovery and the development of its human resources and social
sectors. The basic economic and social data for Chad are set out in
Annexes 1 and 2.
II. THE ECONOMY AND THE ADJUSTMENT PROCESS
A. Socioeconomic Trends and the Adiustment Process
2.01      During the first 18 years following independence in 1960, Chad
experienced steady economic growth, largely as a result of incentives for
its main export crop, cotton, which in n.ost years generated two-thirds of
export earnings and was an important source of government revenues.
Favorable weather conditions sustained adequate levels of food production.
Aggregate production reached its peak level in 1977, raising GDP per capita
to US$157. A drastic downturn began in 1978 as the country struggled
through a series of external and natural events: decline of the world price



- 2 -
for cotton, severe drought, and unrest. The 1979-82 war destroyed most of
the country's physical and administrative infrastructure and reduced its
economic activities, while the prolonged drought decimated the livestock
herd, reduced food production by half in 1984, and displacrd a large
portion of the population--mainly from the northern and Saharan zones--to
the south and tc the capital city, N'Djamena, as well as to neighboring
countries. These developments, together with collapse of the world cotton
market in 1985, caused a severe contraction of the economy. Real GDP has
remained below the 1977 level, while the population is estimated to have
grown by about 2.22 p.a. during the past 10 years. The country's past
economic performance is analyzed in a Country Economic Memorandum (Report
No. 6785-CD), distributed to the Executive Directors in October 1987.
2.02      Adiustment measures.  With economic and social recovery the
country's paramount need, in 1986 the Government took the first steps
toward rebuilding the devastated physical infrastructure and restoring the
economy's productive capacity. Measures to stimulate domestic production
were supplemented by measures to improve resource allocation in the
economy. The stabilization and adjustment programs first focused on the
cottor. sector and public finances and then on the key sectors of livestock,
transport, and education. The adjustment programs are described in a
Policy Framework Paper (PFP 1989-91). They were supported by an IMF
Structural Adjustment Facility (SAF) program in 1987-88 and an IDA
Financial Rehabilitation Credit (FRC - Cr. 1945-CD) approved by the
Executive Directors in July 1988. A second PFP (1989-91) and a new IMF SAF
program for 1989 were approved in June 1989. A third PFP (1990-92) and a
third-year arrangement under the IMF SAF have been agreed with the
Government, and Board Presentation is expected before end of FY90.
2.03      Specifically, the adjustment programs involve:
(a) Restructuring of the cotton sector under a three-year program
(1988/89-1990/91) to improve the sector's economic and financial
viability by building on the achievements of an emergency
program (1986/87-1987/88) which reduced costs and losses within
the existing incentive system and institutional framework. The
three-year program has introduced fundamental reforms to improve
efficiency and reduce financial risks in the cotton industry.
Under the emergency program, one-half of COTONTCHAD's work force
(about 1,250 workers) has been laid off.
(b) Improvement of the country's public finances, which face a
number of constraints: e.g., though the parastatal sector is
small and the civil service is not over-sized, neither is well
managed; and the fiscal base, eroded by the cotton crisis, is
weak and heavily dependent on petroleur and other import taxes.
Chad's fiscal situation is also complicated by high security and
defense expenditures, as well as ay the need to reform the
public sector.
(c) Sectoral reforms, starting with (i) livestock and rural
development (strengthening of research and extensioin services,



cost-recovery for water charges), (ii) education (rehabilitation
of primary education, promotion of community participation,
teacher retraining), and (iii) transport (road repair and
maintenance, decentralization, private sector promotion).
2.04      Social Dimensions of Adjustment.  The adjustment programs are
genera.ly on target after the first three years of implementation.  GDP
fluctuated widely in 1987-89, due to variable weather conditions, with an
average growth of 5 p.a. in real terms. But with an estimated population
growth of 2.22 p.a. and an expected 3Z growth of GDP in real terms, per
capita income will increase only marginally over the next few years. In
addition, although the programs will also improve social services by
providing a modest increase in the current expenditures of the human
resources ministries, public resources for still largely unmet basic needs
will remain strained because of the Government's severe budgetary
constraints. The impact on various population groups of the adjustment
programs, supported by IDA, is difficult to estimate. A number of positive
effects on the incomes of cotton producers, herders and traders are
expected from measures to improve the cotton sector's efficiency, decrease
agricultural input costs, and remove monopolies and fixed tariffs in the
transport sector. The latter measure is also expected to affect the
privileged groups that benefitted from the now discontinued regulatory
practices in that sector. However, the reform measures would also
adversely affect small producers in the cotton zone living in areas of
marginal potential where production has been or may be discontinued, as
well as wage earners in the modern sector, who will continue to face the
effects of a contracting public enterprise sector over the next years. The
social consequences of the events of the past decade, compounded by the
limited prospects for rapid economic growth, thus make improvement of an
already precarious social situation even more elusive without external and
well-targeted assistance.
B. Poverty and Social Development Issues
2.05      Ia Chad, one cannot isolate the consequences of the external and
natural events from those more directly linked to the requirements of
financial stabilization and economic adjustment. What is clear, however,
is that the series of disasters the country experienced has had a
profoundly negative impact on all social groups, although some have
recovered, or are likely to recover, more quickly than others. In a
preliminary assessment of the varying social situation, one population
group--the numerous residents of N'Djamena--has been identified as being
especially at risk, not only because they suffered particularly from
extensive destruction in the capital city, but also because many of them
recently migrated to N'Djamena and are living under precarious conditions.
The remaining population groups -- part_.cularly those from the drought- and
strife-stricken regions, many of whom migrated, perhaps permanently, to
urban areas -- have likewise suffered, albeit to a lesser extent, fron a
temporary lack of productive activities and public services and from a lack
of transportation and infrastructure.



- 4 -
2.06      Since 1986, a number of macroeconomic and sectoral studies have
been undertaken, including a sample household survey in August 1988 of
living conditions in N'Djamena, a preliminary country assessment on poverty
issues, and a small pilot study in the Sahelian zone--all three financed by
the Bank's Social Dimensions of Adjustment (SDA) Regional Facility. These
studies provide partial but useful data on the social situation and on
inequalities in the coverage of basic needs. Their findings confirmed (a)
N'Djamena's precarious social situation; and (b) the need to undertake a
comprehensive data collection program before a more complete assessment of
the poverty profile in Chad can be obtained and further targeted programs
identified, especially in rural areas. The main available data are
summarized below, with emphasis on employment, income structure, social
services, and the housing situation.
2.07      Employment.  Chad's population was estimated at 5.5 million in
1989, with a total labor force of about at 1.8 million. The modern sector
(comprising the public sector and some industries) employs about 70,000, or
4Z of that total. Government employment was estimated at 24,800 in January
1989, which represents a rapid increase from the estimated 14,000 in 1977.
As of 1986, the six largest industries (cotton, sugar, textiles, beer,
cigarettes, and soft drinks) employed 6,000. About 100 small- and medium-
scale enterprises, providing some 5,000 jobs, were active before the war,
but it is not known how many have resumed operations. The level of
unemployment is difficult to estimate but is assumed to be high; for
example, in the 1988 household survey in N'Djamena an estimated 33Z of
males older than 15 were unemployed. In the working population, self-
employment among women is high: 83Z, compared to 46Z for men. With respect
to wage earners, 30Z are civil servants and 202 work for a public or
private enterprise; the remaining 502 are engaged in familial activities.
Chadian city dwellers, like those in other African cities, compensate for
limited opportunities for formal wage employment by expanding a wide range
of "informal" activities: i.e., those outside the official and financial
framework, such as small-scale construction, carpentry, metal work,
tailoring, rug and basket weaving, leather tanning, pottery, food
processing, and marketing. In light of the high level of open or disguised
unemployment in Chad, the Government recognizes the necessity to expand
informal activities through the promotion of micro-entrepreneurs.
2.08      Incuute structure.  During the period 1983-89, sectoral
allocation of income sources averaged 43Z of GDP for agriculture, including
livestock; 20Z for industry, including mining and energy; 33Z for trade,
transport and private services; and 4Z for public services. This is in
contrast with the low percentage of the labor force occupied in the modern
sector, but does not allow any confirmation of wide income disparities
between rural and urban families, especially in a still traditional society
where income transfers, estimated at 12.7Z of total expenditures in the
1988 household survey in N'Djamena, remain important. The same survey
indicates that 62Z of the active population (90Z for women) work in the
trade and service sectors; this confirms the importance of the private
tertiary sector, especially informal activities, for generating monetary
income. The findings, however, do not identify income differentials among
urban population groups. From the preliminary information available (para



- 5 -
2.06) and pending the results of further studies, such as those to be
undertaken in the proposed project, the qualitative impression is that
there are important groups at risk in N'Djamena, in particular because the
effect of food supply and price fluctuations is more pronounced on the
urban poor. The situation of the rural groups is not sufficiently
documented to identify which ones could be seriously at risk, but most of
the rural population is presumed to be not as immediately vulnerable as the
urban. With good rainy seasons, food production in the south increased
between 1985 and 1989, and herds are being reconstituted.
2.09      Population issues.  Indicators of Chad's high levels of maternal
and infant mortality call for the development of safe motherhood, child-
spacing and preventive programs (in particular vaccinations). A law dating
trom colonial times forbids the use, distribution or advertising of
contraceptives; but non-enforcement of the law has led to sales of
contraceptives, at high cost, in private pharmacies and to abortions
performed illegally. In light of the long-term impact of population-
related issues on socioeconomic development and the country's health
status, and despite their political sensitivity, the Government has
initiated programs to promote better public understanding of these issues,
analyze requirements for the legal provision of contraceptives and prepare
new regulations, provide training for health workers on family planning
methods, and assess the possibility of creating local associations to
promote family planning. A seminar on birth spacing and family planning
was held in N'Djamena in November 1988 to sensitize political and religious
leaders to the need for these programs and their importance in improving
maternal and child health. Recommendations were made during the seminar to
integrate family planning into national health and social programs.
2.10      Health.  The health status of Chad's nopulation is poor in
comparison with the averages for Sub-Saharan Africa (SSA), as evidenced by
health indicators given in the Long-term Perspective Study for Sub-Saharan
Africa (1989). Chad has an infant mortality rate of 134 per 1,000 live
births, versus 114 in SSA; a crude death rate of 20 per 1,000, versus 16;
and a life expectancy at birth of 46 years, versus 51 years, male and
female combined. Trauma, diarrheal diseases, respiratory infections, and
fever (usually caused by malaria) are the major reasons for consultations
in the health facilities. The health care system remains limited, with
services unevenly distributed; in some areas foreign assistance, mainly
from NGOs, has allowed a minimal level of services to be maintained.
Moreover, most public primary health care (PHC) facilities are under-
utilized because of a lack of medications and confidence in the services
they provide. Only about 24Z of people seeking medical assistance visit
these facilities, and this leads to overcrowding in the eight hospitals, as
is the case in N'Djamena, where the Central Hospital is the main source of
health care. Although AIDS is not yet a major problem in Chad, a medium-
term plan has been developed by the Government in collaboration with the
World Health Organization (WHO).
2.11      Nutrition.  The lack of a national information system precludes
a comprehensive assessment of nutrition in Chad. There is a famine early
warning system, supported by the European Development Fund (EDF), that



identifies groups at nutritional risk in N'Djamena and a few Sahelian
zones. Ad-hoc nutrition surveys, conducted since 1987, indicate that acute
malnutrition exists in most of the surveyed areas; in N'Djamena, for
example, the severity of the nutritional situation in 1984-85 is shown in
these findings: 10.32 of children 0-5 years old, weighing less than 80? of
the median group for their age, suf:.ered from malnutrition; 352 of
children's hospitalizations are for malnutrition and for dehydration
resulting from frequent bouts of diarrhea; and nearly 60Z of deaths among
children admitted to the Central Hospital's Pediatric Unit occurred within
the first two days of hospitalization, with many suffering from severe
malnutrition in addition to their diagnosed illness. Since 1983,
prevention-oriented nutrition services have been provided by Social Centers
under the Ministry of Social Affairs and Women's Development (MASPF).
These centers are primarily responsible for referring children to the
Pediatric Unit, but it has received less than 1Z of its patients from the
centers. An evaluation by the Ministry confirmed that the centers'
inability to perform their nutrition and referral tasks is mainly due to
shortages of trained staff, essential drugs, food, and growth-monitoring
equipment. The Government is preparing a food security strategy, with the
assistance of the Food and Agriculture Organization (FAO) and the Bank.
2.12      Education and training.  The education and training sector
suffered severely during the events that left much of the country's public
infrastructure damaged or destroyed. With many school-s destroyed and most
equipment and teaching material lost, both access to and quality of
education have declined. At the initiative of parents' associations
determined to provide some education for their children, a spontaneous
school movemnent has emerged; however, the enrollment ratio in primary
education in Chad remains low (49Z in 1988/89). Rehabilitation programs
for primary schools are under way, some financed by IDA under the Education
Rehabilitation Project (Cr. 1950-CD, approved in July 1988). There is also
urgent need to rehabilitate/upgrade the vocational training institutions
and provide training assistance to both the public and private sectors,
including the informal sector (para 2.07), to help meet the country's
requirements for skillea personnel. Lack of an adequate and well-trained
labor force not only impairs the efficiency of any productive investment,
but also hampers the overall reconstruction and recovery effort.
2.13      Housing and sanitation.  For most of the urban population,
living conditions are extremely poor, as indicated by the 1988 household
survey (para 2.06). This is largely due to the widespread destruction and
deterioration of the cities' housing and sanitation infrastructure,
especially in N'Djamena, which sustained some of the worst war damage.
Before a housing rehabilitation program can be undertaken, a minimal level
of sanitation infrastructure must be achieved. Improving sanitation for
the capital city's half million residents, who presently have no system of
sanitation and garbage collection, is therefore an absolute priority.
Waste-water is inadequately drained, leading to contamination of
underground water sources that supply much of the city's drinking water.
In addition, storm and sewer drainage is a serious problem during the rainy
season, and the absence of storm drainage causes extensive flooding.
Health hazards resulting from this situation are enormous, but so far



little has been done to alleviate the sanitation and drainage problems
except for sporadic efforts by NGOs to mobilize communities to improve
their living conditions.
C. The Financial Sector
2.14      The Chadian financial sector is of limited size and has been
severely affected by the events of the past decade. There are now three
active financial institutions: the national office cf the central bank of
the Central African monetary zone, the Banque des Etats de l'Afrique
Centrale, and two commercial banks, the Banque Internationale pour
l'Afrique au Tchad and the Banque Tchadienne de Credit et de Depots. Three
other banks--a commercial bank, a development bank, and a savings bank--
have remained inactive since their closing in 1980. The active banking
sector is very shallow; it has a narrow clientele base, comprising mainly
government agencies, international aid agencies and major enterprises, and
its activities are practically confined to the provision of short-term
credit.
2.15      The 'rivate sector, consisting basically of small and medium
enterprises (SMEs), has barely access to formal credit through the
conventional financial system. The two active banks have not been able to
provide meaningful financing to SMEs out of the two lines of credit made
available to them in 1985 by the French Caisse Centrale de Cooperation
Economique (CCCE) and the European Investment Bank (EIB), for three main
reasons. These banks are already saddled with a large number of defaulted
loans and therefore would not like to expand their exposure to a category
of borrowers considered as carrying high risks. Loan-qualifying criteria
(provision of audited financial statements, marketing analysis, collateral
and life insurance) are particularly strict in the Chadian context and
cannot be met by the vast majority of SME loan applicants. Financing
requirements in Chad for SMEs are relatively low, and lending to such
businesses would not be cost effective because of the intensive appraisal
and supervision work involved.
D. Ongoing Programs and Sectoral Issues
2.16      Until recently, most of the social services in Chad were
maintained by donor emergency programs and by NGOs and in some of the
regions by communities. The Government is strengthening and in some cases
rebuilding its administration. IDA in particular, under a Technical
Assistance Project (Cr. 8270-CD), is providing support to the ministries
responsible for economic and financial planning. But the ministries
concerned with social issues are poorly staffed, lack sufficient trained
personnel following the decade of disruptions in government operat'ons, and
their newly recruited staff are inadequately supervised; hence, for some
time, this situation calls for strong external assistance and special
project implementation arrangements. NGO participation, for example, could
expand the presently limited absorptive capacity of private and
governmental institutions.



2.17      The NGO community has played a critical role in providing
support during the country's crisis period by expanding their activities so
as to serve as the main delivery channel between donors and the people.
Now that the Government has reasserted its role and the NGO community has
turned its focus from emergency relief operations to development-oriented
programs, there is need for the Government and the NGOs to coordinate their
actions and seek consistent objectives. NGO operations in CLad are
multiple, ranging from intervention in crop diversification, education,
health, and small-scale enterprise development to the training of local
extension agents, project managers and government counterpart agents and to
the promotion of community participation in development. Most concentrate
on integrated rural development in small rural zones; by contrast, few
assistance programs have been launched so far in N'Djamena. In the health
sector, NGOs have provided valuable support to the Ministry of Public
Health, in particulst by extending their activities at the regional level;
for example, Medecins sans Frontieres (MSF) has provided all health care
services in nine of Chad's 13 regions, while church-affiliated missions
have been active in the other four. In the industrial sector, NGOs have
been active in promoting small- and medium-scale enterprises; a USAID-
financed project, executed by VITA (Volunteers in Technical Assistance),
promotes access of these enterprises to short-term credit facilities,
together with managerial assistance, mostly in N'Djamena. The USAID-
financed program has played a significant role in filling the gap in
providing productive credit for SMEs. In the urban sector, NGOs have
undertaken sanitation projects and are actively involved in community
sensitization and participation; examples are OXFAM and the Association
Internationale contre la Faim (AICF), with recent activities concentrated
in some of N'Djamena's poorest neighborhoods.
2.18      The Government is committed to pursuing a comprehensive social
policy that aims to develop human resources, expand basic social services
and alleviate poverty, starting with urgently needed actions in spite of
the weakness of its administrative capacity. But before implementing
large-scale sectoral development operations there is immediate need to (a)
evaluate the numerous actions in progress in many regions of the country,
financed by external assistance which sometimes uses divergent methods; (b)
assess basic needs and the available resources for meeting them; and (c)
reach agreement on coherent, long-term objectives. After the priority
given to primary education (para 2.12) with the assistance of several
donors, studies are being undertaken to define a more global strategy for
the education/training sector. The Government also needs to define an
employment and private sector strategy as well as the institutional
responsibilities; at present, only an NGO-run promotion program for small
enterprises exists in N'Djamena (para 2.17). With respect to nutrition,
FAO has taken the lead in helping the Government prepare a food security
strategy. In the health sector, a sectoral study is underway with AfDB
financing. In the meantime, regional health management is being
strengthened with donor assistance; the success of these prefecture-level
programs is essential in developing a sound basic health care system. The
country is divided into 13 regions or prefectures, and three ongoing health
projects, financed by EDF, Italy and Belgium, now cover or will cover all
regions except Tandjile and the city of N'Djamena. But there is need to



- 9 -
clearly define the roles of the Ministries of Public Health (MSP) and
Social Affairs and Women's Development (MASPF) as well as to coordinate
their staff and field activities. Social services (nutrition programs,
community and family well-being, women's educatior.. infant protection) are
poorly defined (para 2.11) and field actions overlap those of the health
services. With respect to urban development, the Ministry of Regional
Urban Planning and Housing (MATUH) was created in May 1989, and N'Djamena's
municipal services are being strengthened with French assistance; a master
plan for rehabilitating N'Djamena's sanitation infrastructure is under
preparation with AfDB financing. The Government intends to organize a
donors' meeting on human resources development in 1990.
E. The Bank Group's Role
2.19      The Bank Group resumed its activities in Chad in mid-1985.
Since then, nine operations, totalling US$241.9 million equivalent, have
been approved as of April 1990. IDA's assistance aims at strengthening
economic management and aid coordination and supporting macroeconomic
policy reforms in close coordination with the IMF. IDA's development
strategy also aims at supporting the rehabilitation of, and promotion of
investments in, the infrastructure (i.e., roads, urban development) and
productive sectors (i.e., agriculture, livestock, energy, and the private
sector). A transport sector adjustment/investment project, based on
infrastructure rehabilitation and on liberalization of the transport
industry, was approved in April 1989 (Cr. 2007-CD). Emphasis in the
strategy is also given to human resources development, starting with the
Education Rehabilitation Project (para 2.12). A Bank Resident Mission,
opened in N'Djamena in January 1989, is strengthening the Bank Group's
dialogue with both the Government and the donor community.
2.20      Rationale for IDA's involvement.  Addressing the basic needs of
the disadvantaged population groups is a high priority in the Government's
development and reconstruction effort. In addition to helping the
Government improve economic efficiency, raise production in key sectors and
develop human resources, IDA could play a key role in (a) supporting the
Government's efforts in identifying and implementing programs targeted at
the population groups at risk, taking into account the short- and medium-
term budgetary and economic constraints; (b) helping to mobilize and
coordinate donor resources for these programs, especially since foreign aid
from which the country benefitted during its critical times has now shifted
from emergency intervention to a growing preference for traditional
investment projects, particularly in agriculture and physical
infrastructure; and (c) assisting the Government in preparing sectoral
strategies that address the long-term constraints to development of the
country's natural and human resources.
2.21      The proposed project would support IDA's objectives in Chad and
the Government's concern for meeting the needs of the population groups (a)
still at risk as a consequence of the prolonged period of socioeconomic
disruptions and for which it has identified priority actions with an
expected immediate impact; and (b) expected to be adversely affected by the
transitional effects of the adjustment process recently initiated, for



- 10 -
which assist#. e would be provided following Chad's requested participation
in the Social Dimensions of Adjustment (SDA) Regional Program, cofinanced
by several donors and under execution by the Bank in Sub-Saharan Africa.
Three small actions have already been financed under the SDA Facility (para
2.06).
2.22      In light of the magnitude of unmet basic needs in Chad and the
Government's budgetary constraints, the project components were selected on
the basis of the following criteria: (a) labor-intensive techniques and/or
local micro-enterprises would be utilized; (b) the impact on government
budgetary expenditures should be minimal; (c) acceptable cost-recovery
schemes and community participation should be tested and promoted; and (d)
measures undertaken in the project should be sustainable after its
completion and be integrated into regular sectoral programs. The project
would serve as a basis for the formulation of broader sectoral actions to
be supported as part of planned lending and sectoral operations.
Furthermore, given the objective of quick implementation in spite of the
weakness of public administration, particularly the acute shortage of
skilled personnel, the project components were also selected with a view to
involving, when feasible, techniques tested by NGOs already active in the
country (para 2.17).
III. THE PROJECT
A. Proiect Obiectives
3.01      The project has three main objectives: (a) stimulating
employment generation by increasing access to training facilities and
productive assets for poor and/or unemployed people; (b) improving health
and living conditions of disadvantaged groups; and (c) reinforcing the
Government's institutional capacity to design and monitor programs to
improve social conditions.
B. Summary of Project Composition
3.02      The project would be implemented over a five-year period and
comprises four main components:
(a) Stimulation of employment generation by providing (i) credit
assistance and management support for small and micro-
enterprises, (ii) technical assistance tailored to the needs of
micro-enterprises, and (iii) assistance to laid-off public
employees.
(b) Improvement of primary health care and social services by
(i) restructuring and expanding the network of these services in
N'Djamena, including the construction and operation of three new
integrated health-social centers, three dispensaries and three
maternity centers; and (ii) strengthening the regional health
services in Tandjile, a prefecture in the cotton zone.



- 11 -
(c) Improvement of N'Djamena's sanitation infrastructure by
(i) rehabilitating the drainage system in two flood-prone areas
where a high percentage of low-income families live, and (ii)
improving public hygiene through increased community
participation and use of labor-intensive public works
techniques.
(d) Strengthening of social policy planning by (i) undertaking small
sample household surveys in the cotton zone and three cities, an
integrated nationwide household survey, and socioeconomic
studies, focusing first on public resource allocation, basic
services coverage, cost recovery and food security issues; and
(ii) assisting the Government in applying the findings of these
surveys and studies, together with experience gained in
implementing the above-described components, to develop policies
and actions targeted at vulnerable population groups.
3.03      The components to stimulate employment generation, improve
health and social services, and improve N'Djamena's sanitatiGn
infrastructure have been designed to meet the project's objective of
responding as quickly as feasible to the social situation; they are
expected to be implemented over a three-year period. On the other hand,
implementation of an integrated household survey and related studies would
require at least five years, with completion expected by end of 1995.
C. Project Description
1. Stimulation of Employment Generation
3.04      This component comprises three main sub-components:  (a) credit
assistance and management support for micro-enterprises, mostly those in
the informal sector (i.e., with very small productive activities outside
the realm of official and financial institutions); (b) technical assistance
tailored to their immediate needs; and (c) assistance to personnel in the
cotton sector laid off as a result of the sector's restructuring and those
to be laid-off over the next years in public enterprises (para 2.03).
Promotion of small and micro-enterprises to create employment and generate
income (paras 2.07 and 2.08) is particularly important in the context of
the Chadian economy. However, the financial institutions are still
recovering from the events of the past decade, and for all practical
purposes are unable to engage in credit operations with small entrepreneurs
(paras 2.14-2.15). A study to identify ways to improve the performance of
the banking sector will be undertaken during 1990 as part of the measures
to be un ertaken in the PFP 1990-92. In the interim, the USAID-financed
ongoing program for small entrepreneurs (para 2.17) would be continued and
expanded under the project through the first sub-component. This and the
second sub-component would be mutually supportive in helping very small
entrepreneurs to select adequate equipment, improve efficiency and quality
control, and widen their activities such as by creating cooperatives for
group purchasing and production planning. Experience gained in this
component would support the development of a coherent and long-term



- 12 -
strategy that focuses on the promotion of micro-enterprises and delineation
of the role of the Government (para 2.18). This component is described
below and further detailed in Annex 3-1. Implementation arrangements are
set out in paras. 4.09-4.11.
3.05      Credit assistance and management support.  This sub-component
would (a) assist small and micro-enterprises in strengthening their
capabilities for preparing financially viable projects and in improving
their managerial and financial performance; and (b) through a credit
scheme, enable them to obtain small loans for expanding their operations
until they become eligible for standard bank credit. Credit eligibility
criteria for the USAID-financed ongoing program were compared with the
existing interest rate structure and normal banking costs. Fees for
banking assistance and credit request preparation have been added, and
lending terms were finalized during negotiations (para 4.09). The credit
scheme would offer three credit windows. The first credit window, newly
established under the proposed credit scheme, would provide c.edits not
exceeding US$1,000 equivalent, together with assistance to address the
enterprises' management and investment needs; these include assessing how
to improve efficieucy and product quality, conduct day-to-day business,
start up new ventures such as group purchasing and planning, and provide
mutual-type credit guarantees. As this credit and assistance program would
be an innovative one for Chad, implementation would be closely monitored.
The techniques to be applied and an outreach plan for attracting small
entrepreneurs would undergo three years of testing under the project. The
second credit window, already established under the current USAID-financed
program, would offer credits ranging from US$1,000 up to US$25,000
equivalent, and would expand credit assistance for small operations that
require more formal management techniques. The third credit window would
offer credits ranging from US$25,000 to US$50,000 equivalent to finance
larger operations with possible cofinancing by local banks; the Banque
Tchadienne de Credit et de Depots (BTCD) has already signed an agreement Lo
do so.  Gross credit commitments vould amount to US$1.6 million over three
years; and 530 micro-enterprises are expected to benefit from the first
credit window, 137 small enterprises from the second, and 12 from the
third, Total cost of this sub-component is estimated at US$2.9 million
equivalent, including US$0.6 million for the incremental credit
requirements.
3.06      Technical assistance for micro-enterprises.  For Chad, this sub-
component represents an experimental approach in the promotion of micro-
enterprises. With the vocational training institutions severely damaged,
disorganized and not yet rebuilt (para 2.12), it would fill an interim need
by providing the enterprises with access to appropriate equipment and
technologies. It would also develop additional areas of support for the
enterprises; e.g., helping them to improve their technical knowledge,
product design and quality control; share experience with other small
enterprises and develop joint projects: and set up cooperative information
systems for the transfer of micro-enterprise technology. This technical
assistance would focus on training with appropriate machinery,
demonstration of techniques, equipment and input supply, research and
development, and documentation. The training support would consist of



- 13 -
practical courses organized in workshop facilities managed by NGOs or
public entities; initially, it would concentrate on the metal work and
mechanical subsectors. It is expected that the instructor staff providing
the training could become the nucleus of an autonomous technical network to
continue technical assistance for the enterprises after project completion,
under an institutional arrangement to be decided at that time by the
Government. Total cost of this sub-component is estimated at US$1.4
million equivalent, including US$0.6 million for specialist services and
US$0.4 million for equipment and technology transfer.
3.07      Assistance to laid-off public sector employees.  This sub-
component would strengthen the capacity of the National Department of Labor
(ONAMO) to provide assistance for personnel affected by the reduction of
severe overstaffing in COTONTCHAD and the Office National de Developpement
Rural (ONDR) during the restructuring of the cotton sector (para 2.03).
Strengthening of ONAMO's capacity in this area is crucial in view of the
expected increased demand for its services arising from the adjustment
process. Although severance payments have been made to most of the laid-
off employees, they along with all other laid-off public employees would be
offered free, individual counselling and assistance to explore job and
alternative employment opportunities. About 3,000 people are expected to
benefit from the counselling program during the project period. In the
counselling sessions, the person's skill level and job potential would be
tested and job market opportunities, as well as the possibilities of
retraining or setting up a small business, explored (e.g., by making use of
the above-described credit and technical assistance programs). A permanent
inquiry/referral system on job availability in the formal and, if feasible,
the informal sector would be developed and tested, with a view to becoming
an integral part of ONAMO's activities. Total cost of this sub-component
is estimated at US$0.4 million equivalent.
2. Improvement of Primary Health Care and Social Services
3.08      This component comprises two sub-components: (a) restructuring
and expanding the network of primary health care (PHC) and social services
in N'Djamena and improving coordination of their activities (para 2.18);
and (b) strengthening the regional health services in Tandjile, in the
eastern part of the cotton zone. Staff training, essential drug supply
with cost recovery, and a restructuring program to improve the planning,
management and monitoring of PHC in these two areas are included in both
sub-components. Problems linked with community participation in improving
health conditions (or decreasing health hazards) would also be addressed in
the sanitation infrastructure component (para 3.11). Implementation of the
component to improve health and social services is expected to provide
valuable experience in dealing with PHC issues, particularly information on
who has access to basic services. This component, as in the case of the
one to stimulate employment generation, would not only help Chad to improve
sector development prospects, but also identify issues and provide the
experience needed to prepare better-targeted strategies and action
programs. The health sector study recently initiated with the assistance
of AfDB (para 2.18) will help develop a long-term national health strategy.



- 14 -
A free-standing health and population project is included in IDA's future
assistance program. Activities to be supported under this component are
described below and further detailed in Annex 3-2; implementation
arrangements are set out in paras 4.12-4.13.
3.09      Restructuring and expansion of N'Diamena's PHC and social
services network. This sub-component would be implemented through four
related actions. First, the PHC and social services network would be
rehabilitated and expanded to permit the urban population (estimated at
540,000) to have easier access to a well-functioning social and/or health
center. Addition of a first set of strategically placed facilities would
improve access for the population within the catchment area of each
facility, from 45Z in 1988 to an estimated 75Z by the end of the project;
but more important, their utilization rate is expected to about double
because of a more reliable delivery system. Four health andlor social
centers would be renovated and three integrated health-social centers and
three dispensaries built, equipped and staffed to provide basic health care
and social services. Training and supervision programs would be provided
for health and social personnel. A more efficient way to address health-
related and social issues through community development approaches would be
developed in the three integrated health-social centers by undertaking a
coordinated program of training, work planning and community work involving
the staff of both the Ministry of Public Health (MSP) and the Ministry of
Social Affairs and Women's Development (MASPF) at the service and
supervisory levels. Second, methods would be developed and tested to (a)
improve and better integrate the content and quality of basic services by
defining better conceived and targeted service packages (objectives,
performance standards, training, and reference and supervisory materials
for basic services) for nutrition education (using existing food
distribution programs), safe motherhood and birth spacing, maternal and
child health (MCH), and simple preventive and curative care; (b) allocate
and prescribe essential drugs more effectively; (c) implement a cost-
recovery program based on a system of fixed fees to be charged for each new
episode of illness, entitling the patient to health care services and
essential drugs; (d) develop a coordinated team approach for identifying
and addressing health-related problems faced by the target communities; and
(e) through an outreach program, promote their participation in the
improvement of their living environment. The proposed cost-recovery scheme
would be part of an overall effort by the Government to develop a
nationwide cost-recovery system, with support from EDF, UNICEF, IDA and
other donors. Third, the referral system would be reviewed and made more
efficiez.t, particularly through better communication (including
transportation) and coordination between peripheral facilities and the
Central Hospital services, particularly pediatrics, which are over-
burdened with trivial cases. Three 16-bed maternity centers would be built
to permit safer normal delivery; problem cases would be referred to the
Central Maternity, which should then become less crowded and more efficient
by focusing its efforts on complicated or high-risk deliveries. Fourth,
overall management of the urban health and social services would be
strengthened by dividing N'Djamena's urban areas into districts, with a
physician and a social worker in charge of regularly planning and
coordinating the action programs of all health and social centers within



- 15 -
each urban district. Total cost of this sub-component is estimated at
US$5.3 million equivalent, including US$1.2 million for essential drugs.
US$1.7 million for construction/rehabilitation of health facilities, and
US$1.1 million for specialist services.
3.10       Strengthening of regional health services in Tandlile.  This
sub-component to strengthen health services in the Prefecture of Tandjile
(360,000 est. population) would be undertaken in a geographic area
partially affected by the cotton sector adjustment arA not included in any
other health sector assistance program. It would adapt and extend to that
region the approach developed and being implemented under the EDF project
(para 2.18). The sub-component includes: assistance to the regional Chief
Physician in planning, managing and monitoring health services to improve
the delivery of primary health care; staff training (50 qualified and 70
untrained nurses); renovation and equipping of health facilities; and
establishment of an essential drug distribution system, with cost recovery.
The essential drug program for Tandjile would be similar to the one to be
tested in N'Djamena (para 3.09) and includes training in drug distribution
and prescription, and the refurbishing of provincial and sub-regional
pharmacies. The concept of being charged a fixed fee for health care
should be more readily accepted by users in that region because a similar
system exists in health centers operated by NGOs or church-affiliated
groups. Total cost of this sub-component is estimated at US$3.0 million
equivalent. of which US$1.1 million would be for essential drugs, US$0.3
million for rehabilitation and equipping of health facilities, and US$0.7
million for specialist services.
3. Tmprovement of N'Djamena's Sanitation Infrastructure
3.11       This component comprises two sub-components: (a) rehabilitation
of the ca-?ital city's drainage system to prevent flooding during the rainy
season i"i two vulnerable areas, where about 85,000 low-income residents are
couw'entrated; and (b) accompanying measures for improvement of public
hygiene based on low-cost sanitation programs involving labor-intensive
public works techniques and active community participation. In light of
the city's extremely poor living conditions and the serious health hazards
(para 2.13), plus the fact that over the last decade there have been
virtually no investments in urban !nirastructure by either government or
external agencies except limited N.~.O7 assistanco~ to alleviate the worst
conditions (para 2.17), this componenit is given high priority in
N'Djamena's reconstruction and reco,~ry effort. Pending completion of the
master plan for rehabilitating the sanitation infrastructure (para 2.18),
it is important that urgently needed improvements be started under the
project in the areas of the c-ity with the worst health hazards. Moreover,
the techniques for implementing these improvements and for involving
communities and local labor can be tested for replicability and
sustainability in larger programs, with a view to creating maximum
employment and practical training opportunities for the city's unemployed
%v4 unskilled labor. These improvement systems were designed to remain
compatible with those to be proposed under the master plan; hence the
project does not include large and structural rehabilitation of the primary



- 16 -
drainage network. Furthermore, the activities implemented under this
component would be complemented by the surveys and studies to be undertaken
in the component to strengthen social policy formulation. A free-standing
urban project is currcntly included in the Bank's lending program for FY92.
Specific activities are described below and further detailed in
Annex 3-3; implementation arrangements are set out in para 4.14.
3.12      Rehabilitation of che drainage system.  Under this sub-
component the drainage system would be rehabilitated in two areas of
N'Djamenr -Am Rikebe and Hill Leclerc--that experience severe flooding
during the rainy season. Primary infrastructure would be improved by the
temporary cleaning and widening of two collectors to permit water drained
from these areas to be evacuated through them. Secondary infrastructure
would be fully rehabilitated by building a network of drainage ditches,
using labor-intensive techniques to enable small enterprises to
participate. Tertiary infrastructure would be rehabilitated by building a
network of ground ditches around each block, using labor-intensive
techniques and unckilled labor hired daily by the municipal services. The
local residents would be sensitized to the need for keeping the ditches
cleaned out, especially before the start of the rainy season, and for
installing concrete culverts in front of their compounds. Reprofiling and
regrading of some of the streets are expected to improve the efficiency of
the newly rehabilitated drainage system.
3.13      Accompanying measures to improve public hygiene.  Related
measures have been identified with a view to enhancing the project's impact
on sanitation in N'Djamena. One marketplace named 'Cholera' would be
rehabilitated by draining its site, using tertiary ditches connected to the
secondary drainage infrastructure, and by covering it with a stable surface
to keep it clean. An improved and well-maintained marketplace would enable
the city to improve fee collection, thereby increasing its revenues. Other
measures include:  (a) improvement of water supply and pubAic lighting, as
well as the installation of private latrines, in the Hill Leclerc area;
(b) control of mosquitoes through preventive measures such as spraying with
environmentally approved insecticides and keeping water from stagnating in
ditches and in the two drainage collectors in the flood-prone areas; and
(c) improved garbage collection, using techniques already tested in similar
poor areas. These measures to improve public hygiene and living conditions
and reduce health hazards in N'Djamena would be strengthened by a community
participation program to sensitize the local residents to the need for
improving their living and work environment, and to actively involve them
in the sanitation and public hygiene measures.
3.14      In this component, the only direct cost-recovery scheme would be
a market tax for the sanitation services, which if recovered at 1002 of its
face value would pay for local recurrent costs. Beyond that, in order to
improve land tax collection the Government plans to introduce a city-wide
land registration system, with FAC-financed assistance.
3.15      The cost of this component is estimated at US$9.1 million, of
which US$6.1 million would be for civil works. This includes the cost of
jointly appraised activities that would be financed by the United Nations



- 17 -
Capital Development Fund (UNCDF), for an estimated US$3.2 million, and that
include part of the rehabilitation of the drainage system, all new public
utilities, and costs associated with a scheme to promote the installation
of private latrines.
4. Strengthening of Social Policy Planning
3.16      In this component, which would implemeit the SDA national
program prepared by the Government (para 2.21),  -ne Government's capability
to define social policies and programs would be strengthened. This
component would also enable the Government to analyze the impact of policy
measures on vulnerable population groups and to design appropriate
corrective measures, in particular for future adjustment programs. Due to
10 years of administrative disruptions and the lack of updated information,
the country's social situation is not well known; moreover, in addition to
its limited capability for policy formulation and design, the Government
continues to face conflicting priorities in the allocation of its meager
resources. Hence, in Chad the implementation of an SDA-type of program.
such as those underway in other Sub-Saharan countries, requires an approach
that combines external technical assistance with well-planned use of the
country's scarce qualified human resources. in light of all the
constraints, a preliminary set of studies and surveys has been identified
to start a database, complement lessons learned in implementing the other
project components, and determine how to design well-targeted future
adjustment and sectoral programs as well as policy reforms.
3.17      Training, equipment, and short-term technical assistance would
be provided under this component to carry out the socioeconomic studies and
household surveys needed to achieve a better understanding of living
condit-ions and the identification of disadvantaged groups. The action plan
focuses first on five areas: (a) living conditions in the rural, mostly
cotton-growing areas; (b) food security and identification of nutritionally
vulnerable groups; (c) living conditions of urban households and coverage
of essential needs; (d) household budget structures, public resource
allocation and financing of essential services; and (e) promotion of micro-
enterprises and employment generation. The main areas to be addressed are
described in detail in Annex 3-4; implementation arrangements are set out
in para 4.15. A synopsis of this component is shown in Table 3.1. Total
cost is estimated at US$3.6 million.
3.18      Policy formulation and project identification.  In this area,
the following work plan would be finalized in close coordination with other
sector work being undertaken with financing by IDA and other donors: (a)
support for better-designed projects targeted at vulnerable zones or groups
in rural areas; (b) improvement of food security strategy and design of
actions targeted at vulnerable zones or groups; (c) analysis of cost-
recovery issues in education and health, and related arrangements for
ensuring that the most vulnerable groups have access to those services; (d)
definition of a core social expenditure budget for the health and education
sectors: and (e) definition of an overall promotion strategy and
institutional set-up for micro and small enterprises. The findings of the
socioeconomic studies (para 3.19), together with the implementation



- 18 -
experience of the proposed project and that of other projects financed by
other donors or developed by NGOs, will provide the basis for designing
sectoral strategies and policies and developing a project pipeline for
future assistance in private sector promotion, health and nutrition, and
urban development. To be adequately carried out, some of the socioeconomic
studies will have to be preceded by household surveys. Total cost of this
sub-component is estimated at US$0.8 million.
3.19      Socioeconomic studies.  These would aim at assessing the living
conditions of households and hew they cope with meeting their basic needs.
The principal objectives would be to gain a better understanding of the
country's social situation and better analyze inequities in access to
social services. A first set of studies would cover the following topics:
(a) ways to help small growers improve their living conditions and their
efficiency in producing cotton and diversifying crops; (b) food security
and household budget structure to identify vulnerable zones and groups, and
based on their behavior, how to help them cope with food supply variations;
(c) public resources allocation (investment and recurrent expenditures) and
access to essential services and cost-recovery capacity (health, education,
nutrition); (d) living conditions of urban households to identify the most
vulnerable groups and measures for providing better employment
opportunities; and (e) micro-enterprises in the urban sector to assess
their efficiency and propose a better integration into an industrial
development strategy and private sector promotion. Total cost of this sub-
component is estimated at US$0.8 million.
3.20      Household surveys.  These would help the Government to set up a
database on household living conditions and on the general social
situation, to be used as inputs for socioeconomic studies. As the
Government is undertaking a census (1989-91), the first since 1964,
personnel and other resources available for these surveys will be limited.
Hence, only four small surveys would be undertaken during those first three
years, with the focal points being rural areas where cotton growing has
been important and three cities--Moundou, Abeche and N'Djamena--the latter
to establish a comparison with a similar survey in 1988 on the capital city
(para 2.06). Beginning in 1991, an integrated household survey on living
conditions would be undertaken, covering approximately 2,400 households.
The census should establish two important elements to enable this household
survey to be effective: cartography (the basis for any sampling technique)
and resources (trained personnel and physical resources for conducting
field inquiries). The methodology and type of questionnaire would follow
the pattern of similar methodologies being used in other African countries
where SDA-type projects are underway. At the end of the project, building
on the database and on the knowledge gained from the integrated household
survey, the Department of Statistics and Economic and Demographic Studies
(DSEED) shculd be able to continue to carry out a program of low-cost
surveys on household living conditions that would be within the
Government's budgetary capabilities. Total cost of this sub-component is
estimated at US$2.1 million.



- 19 -
